text-align:center; border-top:1px #aaa solid"><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Horizon_2000" title="Horizon 2000">Horizon 2000</a></b></div><div style="width:100%"><div class="noprint" style="float: left; text-align:left;padding-right:0.5em;">&#8592;&#160;<i><a href="/wiki/INTEGRAL" title="INTEGRAL">INTEGRAL</a></i></div><div class="noprint" style="float: right; text-align:right;padding-left:0.5em;"><i><a href="/wiki/Herschel_Space_Observatory" title="Herschel Space Observatory">Herschel</a></i>&#160;&#8594;</div></div>&#160;</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><i><b>Rosetta</b></i> was a <a href="/wiki/Space_probe" title="Space probe">space probe</a> built by the <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European Space Agency</a> launched on 2 March 2004. Along with <i><a href="/wiki/Philae_(spacecraft)" title="Philae (spacecraft)">Philae</a></i>, its lander module, <i>Rosetta</i> performed a detailed study of <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comet</a> <a href="/wiki/67P/Churyumov%E2%80%93Gerasimenko" title="67P/Churyumov&#8211;Gerasimenko">67P/Churyumov&#8211;Gerasimenko</a> (67P).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140630_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140630-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20140805_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20140805-9">[9]</a></sup> During its journey to the comet, the spacecraft <a href="/wiki/Planetary_flyby" title="Planetary flyby">flew by</a> <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroids</a> <a href="/wiki/21_Lutetia" title="21 Lutetia">21 Lutetia</a> and <a href="/wiki/2867_%C5%A0teins" title="2867 &#352;teins">2867 &#352;teins</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-philaemars20070225_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-philaemars20070225-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Auster2010_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Auster2010-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-science28102011_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-science28102011-12">[12]</a></sup> It was launched as the third cornerstone mission of the ESA\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Horizon_2000" title="Horizon 2000">Horizon 2000</a> programme, after <i><a href="/wiki/Solar_and_Heliospheric_Observatory" title="Solar and Heliospheric Observatory">SOHO</a></i>&#160;/ <i><a href="/wiki/Cluster_II_(spacecraft)" title="Cluster II (spacecraft)">Cluster</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/XMM-Newton" title="XMM-Newton">XMM-Newton</a></i>.\n</p><p>On 6 August 2014, the spacecraft reached the comet and performed a series of manoeuvres to eventually <a href="/wiki/Orbit" title="Orbit">orbit</a> the comet at distances of 30 to 10 kilometres (19 to 6&#160;mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAQ_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FAQ-13">[13]</a></sup> On 12 November, its lander module <i>Philae</i> performed the first successful landing on a comet,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-skytel20141112_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-skytel20141112-14">[14]</a></sup> though its battery power ran out two days later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-skytel20141115_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-skytel20141115-15">[15]</a></sup> Communications with <i>Philae</i> were briefly restored in June and July 2015, but due to diminishing solar power, <i>Rosetta</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> communications module with the lander was turned off on 27 July 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-farewellphilae_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-farewellphilae-16">[16]</a></sup> On 30 September 2016, the <i>Rosetta</i> spacecraft ended its mission by hard-landing on the comet in its Ma\'at region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newsci20160930_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-newsci20160930-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-space20160930_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-space20160930-18">[18]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The probe was named after the <a href="/wiki/Rosetta_Stone" title="Rosetta Stone">Rosetta Stone</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Stele" title="Stele">stele</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egyptian</a> origin featuring a <a href="/wiki/Decree" title="Decree">decree</a> in three scripts. The lander was named after the <a href="/wiki/Philae_obelisk" title="Philae obelisk">Philae obelisk</a>, which bears a bilingual Greek and Egyptian hieroglyphic inscription.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Mission_overview"><span class="toctext">History</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-3"><a href="#Background"><span class="tocnumber">2.1</span> <span class="toctext">Background</span></a></li>\n<li mission</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-14"><a href="#Instruments"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Instruments</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Nucleus"><span class="tocnumber">3.1</span> <span class="toctext">Nucleus</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16"><a href="#Gas_and_particles"><span class="tocnumber">3.2</span> <span class="toctext">Gas and particles</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#Solar_wind_interaction"><span class="tocnumber">3.3</span> <span class="toctext">Solar wind interaction</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18"><a href="#Search_for_organic_compounds"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Search for organic compounds</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-19"><a href="#Preliminary_results"><span class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span class="toctext">Preliminary results</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20"><a href="#Timeline_of_major_events_and_discoveries"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Timeline of major events and discoveries</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#Public_image"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Public image</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-22"><a href="#Once_upon_a_time..._cartoon"><span class="tocnumber">6.1</span> <span class="toctext"><i>Once upon a time...</i> cartoon</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-23"><a href="#Ambition"><span class="tocnumber">6.2</span> <span class="toctext"><i>Ambition</i></span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-24"><a href="#Media_coverage"><span class="tocnumber">6.3</span> <span class="toctext">Media coverage</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-25"><a href="#Gallery"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Gallery</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-28"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mission_overview">Mission overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rosetta_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Mission overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Comet_67P_on_19_September_2014_NavCam_mosaic.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3500" data-file-width="4400" decoding="async" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Comet_67P_on_19_September_2014_NavCam_mosaic.jpg/220px-Comet_67P_on_19_September_2014_NavCam_mosaic.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Comet_67P_on_19_September_2014_NavCam_mosaic.jpg/330px-Comet_67P_on_19_September_2014_NavCam_mosaic.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Comet_67P_on_19_September_2014_NavCam_mosaic.jpg/440px-Comet_67P_on_19_September_2014_NavCam_mosaic.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Comet_67P_on_19_September_2014_NavCam_mosaic.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/67P/Churyumov%E2%80%93Gerasimenko" title="67P/Churyumov&#8211;Gerasimenko">Comet Churyumov&#8211;Gerasimenko</a> in September 2014 as imaged by <i>Rosetta</i></div></div></div>\n<p><i>Rosetta</i> was launched on 2 March 2004 from the <a href="/wiki/Guiana_Space_Centre" title="Guiana Space Centre">Guiana Space Centre</a> in <a href="/wiki/French_Guiana" title="French Guiana">French Guiana</a> on an <a href="/wiki/Ariane_5" title="Ariane 5">Ariane 5</a> rocket and reached Comet Churyumov&#8211;Gerasimenko on 7 May 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bauer2014_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bauer2014-19">[19]</a></sup> It performed a series of manoeuvres to enter orbit between then and 6 August 2014,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OCM_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-OCM-20">[20]</a></sup> when it became the first spacecraft to orbit a comet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fischer2014-08-06_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fischer2014-08-06-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bauer2014_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bauer2014-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lakdawalla2014-08-15_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lakdawalla2014-08-15-22">[22]</a></sup> (<a href="/wiki/List_of_missions_to_comets" title="List of missions to comets">Previous missions</a> had conducted successful flybys of seven other comets.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-techtimes20141014_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-techtimes20141014-23">[23]</a></sup> It was one of ESA\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_projects_of_the_European_Space_Agency#Horizon_2000_(1985)" title="List of projects of the European Space Agency">Horizon 2000 cornerstone missions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-201401017_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-201401017-24">[24]</a></sup> The spacecraft consisted of the <i>Rosetta</i> orbiter, which featured 12 instruments, and the <i>Philae</i> lander, with nine additional instruments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ESA_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-ESA-25">[25]</a></sup> The <i>Rosetta</i> mission orbited Comet Churyumov&#8211;Gerasimenko for 17 months and was designed to complete the most detailed study of a comet ever attempted. The spacecraft was controlled from the <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Operations_Centre" title="European Space Operations Centre">European Space Operations Centre</a> (ESOC), in <a href="/wiki/Darmstadt" title="Darmstadt">Darmstadt</a>, Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> The planning for the operation of the scientific payload, together with the data retrieval, calibration, archiving and distribution, was performed from the <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Astronomy_Centre" title="European Space Astronomy Centre">European Space Astronomy Centre</a> (ESAC), in <a href="/wiki/Villanueva_de_la_Ca%C3%B1ada" title="Villanueva de la Ca&#241;ada">Villanueva de la Ca&#241;ada</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Madrid" title="Madrid">Madrid</a>, Spain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ESAC2014_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-ESAC2014-27">[27]</a></sup> It has been estimated that in the decade preceding 2014, some 2,000 people assisted in the mission in some capacity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-techrep_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-techrep-28">[28]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2007, <i>Rosetta</i> made a Mars <a href="/wiki/Gravity_assist" title="Gravity assist">gravity assist</a> (flyby) on its way to Comet Churyumov&#8211;Gerasimenko.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mars_fly-by_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mars_fly-by-29">[29]</a></sup> The spacecraft also performed two <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroid</a> flybys.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> The craft completed its flyby of asteroid <a href="/wiki/2867_%C5%A0teins" title="2867 &#352;teins">2867 &#352;teins</a> in September 2008 and of <a href="/wiki/21_Lutetia" title="21 Lutetia">21 Lutetia</a> in July 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-31">[31]</a></sup> Later, on 20 January 2014, <i>Rosetta</i> was taken out of a 31-month <a href="/wiki/Hibernation_(spacecraft)" title="Hibernation (spacecraft)">hibernation mode</a> as it approached Comet Churyumov&#8211;Gerasimenko.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP-20140120new_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-AP-20140120new-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-latimes20140120_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-latimes20140120-33">[33]</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>Rosetta</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> <i>Philae</i> lander successfully made the first soft landing on a <a href="/wiki/Comet_nucleus" title="Comet nucleus">comet nucleus</a> when it touched down on Comet Churyumov&#8211;Gerasimenko on 12 November 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20141112-DCA_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20141112-DCA-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20141112-KC_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20141112-KC-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> On 5 September 2016, ESA announced that the lander was discovered by the narrow-angle camera aboard <i>Rosetta</i> as the orbiter made a low, 2.7&#160;km (1.7&#160;mi) pass over the comet. The lander sits on its side wedged into a dark crevice of the comet, explaining the lack of electrical power to establish proper communication with the orbiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rosetta_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rosetta_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>During the 1986 approach of <a href="/wiki/Halley%27s_Comet" title="Halley\'s Comet">Halley\'s Comet</a>, international space probes were sent to explore the comet, most prominent among them being <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">ESA</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Giotto_(spacecraft)" title="Giotto (spacecraft)"><i>Giotto</i></a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vice20160314_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-vice20160314-38">[38]</a></sup> After the probes returned valuable scientific information, it became obvious that follow-ons were needed that would shed more light on cometary composition and answer new questions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Altwegg2001_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Altwegg2001-39">[39]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Both ESA and <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> started cooperatively developing new probes. The NASA project was the <a href="/wiki/Comet_Rendezvous_Asteroid_Flyby" title="Comet Rendezvous Asteroid Flyby">Comet Rendezvous Asteroid Flyby</a> (CRAF) mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Neugebauer1987_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Neugebauer1987-40">[40]</a></sup> The ESA project was the follow-on Comet Nucleus Sample Return (CNSR) mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwehm1989_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schwehm1989-41">[41]</a></sup> Both missions were to share the <a href="/wiki/Mariner_Mark_II" title="Mariner Mark II">Mariner Mark II</a> spacecraft design, thus minimising costs. In 1992, after NASA cancelled CRAF due to budgetary limitations, ESA decided to develop a CRAF-style project on its own.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moltenbrey2016_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moltenbrey2016-42">[42]</a></sup> By 1993 it was evident that the ambitious sample return mission was infeasible with the existing ESA budget, so the mission was redesigned and subsequently approved by the ESA, with the final flight plan resembling the cancelled CRAF mission: an asteroid flyby followed by a comet rendezvous with in-situ examination, including a lander.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moltenbrey2016_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-Moltenbrey2016-42">[42]</a></sup> After the spacecraft launch, <a href="/wiki/Gerhard_Schwehm" title="Gerhard Schwehm">Gerhard Schwehm</a> was named mission manager; he retired in March 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-techrep_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-techrep-28">[28]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <i>Rosetta</i> mission included generational team management; this allowed mission continuity over the long period of the mission and for special knowledge to be maintained and passed on to future team members. In particular, several younger scientists were brought on as principal science investigators, and regular training sessions were conducted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAQ_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-FAQ-13">[13]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Naming">Naming</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rosetta_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Naming">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The probe was named after the <a href="/wiki/Rosetta_Stone" title="Rosetta Stone">Rosetta Stone</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Stele" title="Stele">stele</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egyptian</a> origin featuring a <a href="/wiki/Decree" title="Decree">decree</a> in three scripts. The lander was named after the <a href="/wiki/Philae_obelisk" title="Philae obelisk">Philae obelisk</a>, which bears a bilingual Greek and Egyptian hieroglyphic inscription. A comparison of its hieroglyphs with those on the Rosetta Stone catalysed the deciphering of the Egyptian writing system. Similarly, it was hoped that these spacecraft would result in better understanding of comets and the early <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-space20140115_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-space20140115-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esaint20040205_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-esaint20040205-44">[44]</a></sup> In a more direct analogy to its namesake, the <i>Rosetta</i> spacecraft also carried a micro-etched pure nickel prototype of the <a href="/wiki/Rosetta_Project" title="Rosetta Project">Rosetta disc</a> donated by the <a href="/wiki/Long_Now_Foundation" title="Long Now Foundation">Long Now Foundation</a>. The disc was inscribed with 6,500 pages of language translations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RosettaDisc_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-RosettaDisc-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RosettaDiscPrototype_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-RosettaDiscPrototype-46">[46]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mission_firsts">Mission firsts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rosetta_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Mission firsts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rosetta_and_Philae_at_comet_(11206660686).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="1534" decoding="async" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Rosetta_and_Philae_at_comet_%2811206660686%29.jpg/170px-Rosetta_and_Philae_at_comet_%2811206660686%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Rosetta_and_Philae_at_comet_%2811206660686%29.jpg/255px-Rosetta_and_Philae_at_comet_%2811206660686%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Rosetta_and_Philae_at_comet_%2811206660686%29.jpg/340px-Rosetta_and_Philae_at_comet_%2811206660686%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rosetta_and_Philae_at_comet_(11206660686).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illustration of <i>Rosetta</i> and <i>Philae</i> at the comet</div></div></div>\n<p>The <i>Rosetta</i> mission achieved many historic firsts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On its way to comet 67P, <i>Rosetta</i> passed through the main <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">asteroid belt</a>, and made the first <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European</a> close encounter with several of these primitive objects. <i>Rosetta</i> was the first spacecraft to fly close to <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>\'s orbit using solar cells as its main power source.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rosettafacts_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-rosettafacts-48">[48]</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>Rosetta</i> was the first spacecraft to orbit a <a href="/wiki/Comet_nucleus" title="Comet nucleus">comet nucleus</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> and was the first spacecraft to fly alongside a comet as it headed towards the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inner_Solar_System" title="Inner Solar System">inner Solar System</a>. It became the first spacecraft to examine at close proximity the activity of a frozen comet as it is warmed by the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>. Shortly after its arrival at 67P, the <i>Rosetta</i> orbiter dispatched the <i><a href="/wiki/Philae_(spacecraft)" title="Philae (spacecraft)">Philae</a></i> lander for the first controlled touchdown on a comet nucleus. The robotic lander\'s instruments obtained the first images from a comet\'s surface and made the first <i><a href="/wiki/In_situ" title="In situ">in situ</a></i> analysis of its composition.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Design_and_construction">Design and construction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rosetta_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Design and construction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <i>Rosetta</i> <a href="/wiki/Satellite_bus" title="Satellite bus">bus</a> was a 2.8&#160;&#215;&#160;2.1&#160;&#215;&#160;2.0&#160;m (9.2&#160;&#215;&#160;6.9&#160;&#215;&#160;6.6&#160;ft) central frame and aluminium honeycomb platform. Its total mass was approximately 3,000&#160;kg (6,600&#160;lb), which included the 100&#160;kg (220&#160;lb) <i>Philae</i> lander and 165&#160;kg (364&#160;lb) of science instruments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> The Payload Support Module was mounted on top of the spacecraft and housed the scientific instruments, while the Bus Support Module was on the bottom and contained spacecraft support subsystems. Heaters placed around the spacecraft kept its systems warm while it was distant from the Sun. <i>Rosetta</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> communications suite included a 2.2&#160;m (7.2&#160;ft) steerable high-gain parabolic dish antenna, a 0.8&#160;m (2.6&#160;ft) fixed-position medium-gain antenna, and two omnidirectional low-gain antennas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nssdc-craft_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-nssdc-craft-51">[51]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Electrical power for the spacecraft came from two solar arrays totalling 64 square metres (690&#160;sq&#160;ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ESA-RO_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-ESA-RO-52">[52]</a></sup> Each solar array was subdivided into five solar panels, with each panel being 2.25&#160;&#215;&#160;2.736&#160;m (7.38&#160;&#215;&#160;8.98&#160;ft). The individual solar cells were made of silicon, 200 &#956;m thick, and 61.95&#160;&#215;&#160;37.75&#160;mm (2.44&#160;&#215;&#160;1.49&#160;in).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAccolti2002_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-DAccolti2002-53">[53]</a></sup> The solar arrays generated a maximum of approximately 1,500 watts at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perihelion" title="Perihelion">perihelion</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAccolti2002_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-DAccolti2002-53">[53]</a></sup> a minimum of 400 watts in hibernation mode at 5.2 AU, and 850 watts when comet operations begin at 3.4 AU.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nssdc-craft_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-nssdc-craft-51">[51]</a></sup> Spacecraft power was controlled by a redundant <a href="/wiki/Terma_A/S" title="Terma A/S">Terma</a> power module also used in the <i><a href="/wiki/Mars_Express" title="Mars Express">Mars Express</a></i> spacecraft,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ingenioren20140119_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ingenioren20140119-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jensen2002_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jensen2002-55">[55]</a></sup> and was stored in four 10-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ampere-hour" title="Ampere-hour">A&#183;h</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NiCd" title="NiCd">NiCd</a> batteries supplying 28 volts to the bus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nssdc-craft_51-2"><a href="#cite_note-nssdc-craft-51">[51]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Main propulsion comprised 24 paired bipropellant 10&#160;<a href="/wiki/Newton_(unit)" title="Newton (unit)">N</a> thrusters,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ESA-RO_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-ESA-RO-52">[52]</a></sup> with four pairs of thrusters being used for <a href="/wiki/Delta-v" title="Delta-v">delta-<i>v</i></a> burns. The spacecraft carried 1,719.1&#160;kg (3,790&#160;lb) of propellant at launch: 659.6&#160;kg (1,454&#160;lb) of <a href="/wiki/Monomethylhydrazine" title="Monomethylhydrazine">monomethylhydrazine</a> fuel and 1,059.5&#160;kg (2,336&#160;lb) of <a href="/wiki/Dinitrogen_tetroxide" title="Dinitrogen tetroxide">dinitrogen tetroxide</a> oxidiser, contained in two 1,108-litre (244&#160;imp&#160;gal; 293&#160;US&#160;gal) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ti6Al4V" title="Ti6Al4V">grade 5 titanium alloy</a> tanks and providing delta-<i>v</i> of at least 2,300 metres per second (7,500&#160;ft/s) over the course of the mission. Propellant pressurisation was provided by two 68-litre (15&#160;imp&#160;gal; 18&#160;US&#160;gal) high-pressure helium tanks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stram2004_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stram2004-56">[56]</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>Rosetta</i> was built in a <a href="/wiki/Cleanroom" title="Cleanroom">clean room</a> according to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/COSPAR" title="COSPAR">COSPAR</a> rules, but "<a href="/wiki/Sterilization_(microbiology)" title="Sterilization (microbiology)">sterilisation</a> [was] generally not crucial since comets are usually regarded as objects where you can find <a href="/wiki/Abiogenesis" title="Abiogenesis">prebiotic</a> <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecules</a>, that is, molecules that are precursors of life, but not living <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganisms</a>", according to Gerhard Schwehm, <i>Rosetta</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> project scientist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esa20020730_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-esa20020730-57">[57]</a></sup> The total cost of the mission was about &#8364;1.3&#160;billion (US$1.8&#160;billion).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature20140717_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-nature20140717-58">[58]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Launch">Launch</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rosetta_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Launch">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Animation_of_Rosetta_trajectory.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="420" data-file-width="560" decoding="async" height="233" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Animation_of_Rosetta_trajectory.gif/310px-Animation_of_Rosetta_trajectory.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Animation_of_Rosetta_trajectory.gif/465px-Animation_of_Rosetta_trajectory.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/bd/Animation_of_Rosetta_trajectory.gif 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Animation_of_Rosetta_trajectory.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Animation of <i>Rosetta</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> trajectory from 2 March 2004 to 9 September 2016 <br/> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:magenta; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<i>Rosetta</i></span>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:lime; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/67P/Churyumov%E2%80%93Gerasimenko" title="67P/Churyumov&#8211;Gerasimenko">67P/Churyumov&#8211;Gerasimenko</a></span>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:Royalblue; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a></span>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:maroon; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a></span>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:Cyan; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/21_Lutetia" title="21 Lutetia">21 Lutetia</a></span>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:Gold; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/2867_%C5%A0teins" title="2867 &#352;teins">2867 &#352;teins</a></span></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rosetta_111106.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="702" data-file-width="1257" decoding="async" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Rosetta_111106.jpg/310px-Rosetta_111106.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Rosetta_111106.jpg/465px-Rosetta_111106.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Rosetta_111106.jpg/620px-Rosetta_111106.jpg 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rosetta_111106.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Trajectory of the <i>Rosetta</i> space probe</div></div></div>\n<p><i>Rosetta</i> was set to be launched on 12 January 2003 to rendezvous with the comet <a href="/wiki/46P/Wirtanen" title="46P/Wirtanen">46P/Wirtanen</a> in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Altwegg2001_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-Altwegg2001-39">[39]</a></sup> This plan was abandoned after the failure of an <a href="/wiki/Ariane_5" title="Ariane 5">Ariane 5</a> carrier rocket during <a href="/wiki/Hot_Bird_7" title="Hot Bird 7">Hot Bird 7</a>\'s launch on 11 December 2002, grounding it until the cause of the failure could be determined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harland2006_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harland2006-59">[59]</a></sup> In May 2003, a new plan was formed to target the comet 67P/Churyumov&#8211;Gerasimenko, with a revised launch date of 26 February 2004 and comet rendezvous in 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esa20030529_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-esa20030529-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature20030522_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-nature20030522-61">[61]</a></sup> The larger mass and the resulting increased impact velocity made modification of the landing gear necessary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>\n</p><p>After two scrubbed launch attempts, <i>Rosetta</i> was launched on 2 March 2004 at 07:17&#160;<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UTC" title="UTC">UTC</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Guiana_Space_Centre" title="Guiana Space Centre">Guiana Space Centre</a> in French Guiana.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esa20040304_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-esa20040304-2">[2]</a></sup> Aside from the changes made to launch time and target, the mission profile remained almost identical. Both co-discoverers of the comet, <a href="/wiki/Klim_Churyumov" title="Klim Churyumov">Klim Churyumov</a> and <a href="/wiki/Svetlana_Gerasimenko" title="Svetlana Gerasimenko">Svetlana Gerasimenko</a>, were present at the spaceport during the launch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esa54156_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-esa54156-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esa54598_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-esa54598-64">[64]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Deep_space_manoeuvres">Deep space manoeuvres</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rosetta_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Deep space manoeuvres">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>To achieve the required velocity to rendezvous with 67P, <i>Rosetta</i> used <a href="/wiki/Gravity_assist" title="Gravity assist">gravity assist</a> manoeuvres to accelerate throughout the inner Solar System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAQ_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-FAQ-13">[13]</a></sup> The comet\'s orbit was known before <i>Rosetta</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> launch, from ground-based measurements, to an accuracy of approximately 100&#160;km (62&#160;mi). Information gathered by the onboard cameras beginning at a distance of 24&#160;million kilometres (15,000,000&#160;mi) were processed at ESA\'s Operation Centre to refine the position of the comet in its orbit to a few kilometres.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>The first <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> flyby was on 4 March 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Montagnon2006_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Montagnon2006-65">[65]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 25 February 2007, the craft was scheduled for a low-altitude flyby of <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>, to correct the trajectory. This was not without risk, as the estimated altitude of the flyby was a mere 250 kilometres (160&#160;mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eoportal_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-eoportal-66">[66]</a></sup> During that encounter, the solar panels could not be used since the craft was in the planet\'s shadow, where it would not receive any solar light for 15 minutes, causing a dangerous shortage of power. The craft was therefore put into standby mode, with no possibility to communicate, flying on batteries that were originally not designed for this task.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> This Mars manoeuvre was therefore nicknamed "The Billion Euro Gamble".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> The flyby was successful, with <i>Rosetta</i> even returning detailed images of the surface and atmosphere of the planet, and the mission continued as planned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-philaemars20070225_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-philaemars20070225-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mars_fly-by_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mars_fly-by-29">[29]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The second Earth flyby was on 13 November 2007 at a distance of 5,700&#160;km (3,500&#160;mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> In observations made on 7 and 8 November, <i>Rosetta</i> was briefly mistaken for a <a href="/wiki/Near-Earth_object" title="Near-Earth object">near-Earth asteroid</a> about 20&#160;m (66&#160;ft) in diameter by an astronomer of the <a href="/wiki/Catalina_Sky_Survey" title="Catalina Sky Survey">Catalina Sky Survey</a> and was given the <a href="/wiki/Provisional_designation_in_astronomy" title="Provisional designation in astronomy">provisional designation</a> <span class="nowrap">2007 VN<sub>84</sub></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Calculations showed that it would pass very close to Earth, which led to speculation that it could impact Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> However, astronomer <a href="/wiki/Denis_Denisenko" title="Denis Denisenko">Denis Denisenko</a> recognised that the trajectory matched that of <i>Rosetta</i>, which the <a href="/wiki/Minor_Planet_Center" title="Minor Planet Center">Minor Planet Center</a> confirmed in an editorial release on 9 November.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-planetary-misidentification_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-planetary-misidentification-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mpec20071109_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-mpec20071109-74">[74]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The spacecraft performed a close flyby of asteroid <a href="/wiki/2867_%C5%A0teins" title="2867 &#352;teins">2867 &#352;teins</a> on 5 September 2008. Its onboard cameras were used to fine-tune the trajectory, achieving a minimum separation of less than 800&#160;km (500&#160;mi). Onboard instruments measured the asteroid from 4 August to 10 September. Maximum relative speed between the two objects during the flyby was 8.6&#160;km/s (19,000&#160;mph; 31,000&#160;km/h).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Signal_received_from_Rosetta_(12055070794).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2832" data-file-width="4256" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Signal_received_from_Rosetta_%2812055070794%29.jpg/220px-Signal_received_from_Rosetta_%2812055070794%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Signal_received_from_Rosetta_%2812055070794%29.jpg/330px-Signal_received_from_Rosetta_%2812055070794%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Signal_received_from_Rosetta_%2812055070794%29.jpg/440px-Signal_received_from_Rosetta_%2812055070794%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Signal_received_from_Rosetta_(12055070794).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Rosetta</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> signal received at <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Operations_Centre" title="European Space Operations Centre">ESOC</a> in <a href="/wiki/Darmstadt" title="Darmstadt">Darmstadt</a>, Germany, on 20 January 2014</div></div></div>\n<p><i>Rosetta</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> third and final flyby of Earth happened on 12 November 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 10 July 2010, <i>Rosetta</i> flew by <a href="/wiki/21_Lutetia" title="21 Lutetia">21 Lutetia</a>, a large <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">main-belt</a> <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroid</a>, at a minimum distance of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7003316800000000000&#9824;</span>3,168<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#177;</span>7.5</span>&#160;km (<span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7003196900000000000&#9824;</span>1,969<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#177;</span>4.7</span>&#160;mi) at a velocity of 15 kilometres per second (9.3&#160;mi/s).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-science28102011_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-science28102011-12">[12]</a></sup> The flyby provided images of up to 60 metres (200&#160;ft) per pixel resolution and covered about 50% of the surface, mostly in the northern hemisphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sierks2011_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sierks2011-77">[77]</a></sup> The 462 images were obtained in 21 narrow- and broad-band filters extending from 0.24 to 1&#160;&#956;m.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-31">[31]</a></sup> Lutetia was also observed by the visible&#8211;near-infrared imaging spectrometer VIRTIS, and measurements of the magnetic field and plasma environment were taken as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_31-3"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sierks2011_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sierks2011-77">[77]</a></sup>\n</p><p>After leaving its hibernation mode in January 2014 and getting closer to the comet <i>Rosetta</i> began a series of eight burns in May 2014. These reduced the relative velocity between the spacecraft and 67P from 775&#160;m/s (2,540&#160;ft/s) to 7.9&#160;m/s (26&#160;ft/s).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OCM_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-OCM-20">[20]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reaction_control_system_problems">Reaction control system problems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rosetta_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Reaction control system problems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In 2006, <i>Rosetta</i> suffered a leak in its <a href="/wiki/Reaction_control_system" title="Reaction control system">reaction control system</a> (RCS).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAQ_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-FAQ-13">[13]</a></sup> The system, which consists of 24 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bipropellant" title="Bipropellant">bipropellant</a> 10-<a href="/wiki/Newton_(unit)" title="Newton (unit)">newton</a> thrusters,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OCM_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-OCM-20">[20]</a></sup> was responsible for fine tuning the trajectory of <i>Rosetta</i> throughout its journey. The RCS operated at a lower pressure than designed due to the leak. While this may have caused the propellants to mix incompletely and burn \'dirtier\' and less efficiently, ESA engineers were confident that the spacecraft would have sufficient fuel reserves to allow for the successful completion of the mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc20140521_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc20140521-78">[78]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Prior to <i>Rosetta</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> deep space hibernation period, two of the spacecraft\'s four <a href="/wiki/Reaction_wheel" title="Reaction wheel">reaction wheels</a> began exhibiting increased levels of "bearing friction noise". Increased friction levels in Reaction Wheel Assembly (RWA)&#160;B were noted after its September 2008 encounter with asteroid &#352;teins. Two attempts were made to relubricate the RWA using an on-board oil reservoir, but in each case noise levels were only temporarily lowered, and the RWA was turned off in mid-2010 after the flyby of asteroid Lutetia to avoid possible failure. Shortly after this, RWA&#160;C also began showing evidence of elevated friction. Relubrication was also performed on this RWA, but methods were found to temporarily increase its operating temperature to better improve the transfer of oil from its reservoir. In addition, the reaction wheel\'s speed range was decreased to limit lifetime accumulated rotations. These changes resulted in RWA&#160;C<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> performance stabilising.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McMahon2017_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-McMahon2017-79">[79]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the spacecraft\'s Deep Space Hibernation flight phase, engineers performed ground testing on a flight spare RWA at the <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Operations_Centre" title="European Space Operations Centre">European Space Operations Centre</a>. After <i>Rosetta</i> exited hibernation in January 2014, lessons learned from the ground testing were applied to all four RWAs, such as increasing their operating temperatures and limiting their wheel speeds to below 1000&#160;rpm. After these fixes, the RWAs showed nearly identical performance data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McMahon2017_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-McMahon2017-79">[79]</a></sup> Three RWAs were kept operational, while one of the malfunctioning RWAs was held in reserve. Additionally, new on-board software was developed to allow <i>Rosetta</i> to operate with only two active RWAs if necessary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAQ_13-4"><a href="#cite_note-FAQ-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sfnow20140129_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-sfnow20140129-80">[80]</a></sup> These changes allowed the four RWAs to operate throughout <i>Rosetta</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> mission at 67P/Churyumov&#8211;Gerasimenko despite occasional anomalies in their friction plots and a heavy workload imposed by numerous orbital changes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McMahon2017_79-2"><a href="#cite_note-McMahon2017-79">[79]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Orbit_around_67P">Orbit around 67P</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rosetta_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Orbit around 67P">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Animation_of_Rosetta_trajectory_around_67P.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="420" data-file-width="560" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Animation_of_Rosetta_trajectory_around_67P.gif/220px-Animation_of_Rosetta_trajectory_around_67P.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Animation_of_Rosetta_trajectory_around_67P.gif/330px-Animation_of_Rosetta_trajectory_around_67P.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Animation_of_Rosetta_trajectory_around_67P.gif/440px-Animation_of_Rosetta_trajectory_around_67P.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Animation_of_Rosetta_trajectory_around_67P.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Animation of <i>Rosetta</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> trajectory around 67P from 1 August 2014 to 31 March 2015 <br/> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:magenta; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<i>Rosetta</i></span>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:lime; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;67P</span></div></div></div>\n<p>In August 2014, <i>Rosetta</i> rendezvoused with the comet <a href="/wiki/67P/Churyumov%E2%80%93Gerasimenko" title="67P/Churyumov&#8211;Gerasimenko">67P/Churyumov&#8211;Gerasimenko</a> (67P) and commenced a series of manoeuvres that took it on two successive triangular paths, averaging 100 and 50 kilometres (62 and 31&#160;mi) from the nucleus, whose segments are <a href="/wiki/Hyperbolic_trajectory" title="Hyperbolic trajectory">hyperbolic escape trajectories</a> alternating with thruster burns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fischer2014-08-06_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fischer2014-08-06-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bauer2014_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bauer2014-19">[19]</a></sup> After closing to within about 30&#160;km (19&#160;mi) from the comet on 10 September, the spacecraft entered actual <a href="/wiki/Orbit" title="Orbit">orbit</a> about it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fischer2014-08-06_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-Fischer2014-08-06-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bauer2014_19-3"><a href="#cite_note-Bauer2014-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lakdawalla2014-08-15_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lakdawalla2014-08-15-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The text near this tag is dated. (November 2014)">needs update</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>The surface layout of 67P was unknown before <i>Rosetta</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> arrival. The orbiter mapped the comet in anticipation of detaching its lander.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esablog230714_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-esablog230714-81">[81]</a></sup> By 25 August 2014, five potential landing sites had been determined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140825_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140825-82">[82]</a></sup> On 15 September 2014, ESA announced Site&#160;J, named <i>Agilkia</i> in honour of <a href="/wiki/Agilkia_Island" title="Agilkia Island">Agilkia Island</a> by an ESA public contest and located on the "head" of the comet,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbcnews20141104_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbcnews20141104-83">[83]</a></sup> as the lander\'s destination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esa20140915_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-esa20140915-84">[84]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Philae_lander"><i>Philae</i> lander</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rosetta_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Philae lander">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Philae_(spacecraft)" title="Philae (spacecraft)">Philae (spacecraft)</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rosetta_and_Philae_(crop).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1050" data-file-width="1400" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Rosetta_and_Philae_%28crop%29.jpg/220px-Rosetta_and_Philae_%28crop%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Rosetta_and_Philae_%28crop%29.jpg/330px-Rosetta_and_Philae_%28crop%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Rosetta_and_Philae_%28crop%29.jpg/440px-Rosetta_and_Philae_%28crop%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rosetta_and_Philae_(crop).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Rosetta</i> and <i>Philae</i></div></div></div>\n<p><i><a href="/wiki/Philae_(spacecraft)" title="Philae (spacecraft)">Philae</a></i> detached from <i>Rosetta</i> on 12 November 2014 at 08:35 UTC, and approached 67P at a relative speed of about 1&#160;m/s (3.6&#160;km/h; 2.2&#160;mph).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> It initially landed on 67P at 15:33 UTC, but bounced twice, coming to rest at 17:33 UTC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-skytel20141112_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-skytel20141112-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-indy20141113_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-indy20141113-86">[86]</a></sup> Confirmation of contact with 67P reached Earth at 16:03 UTC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sfnow20141113_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-sfnow20141113-87">[87]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On contact with the surface, two <a href="/wiki/Harpoon" title="Harpoon">harpoons</a> were to be fired into the comet to prevent the lander from bouncing off, as the comet\'s escape velocity is only around 1&#160;m/s (3.6&#160;km/h; 2.2&#160;mph).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mpg20140121_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-mpg20140121-88">[88]</a></sup> Analysis of telemetry indicated that the surface at the initial touchdown site is relatively soft, covered with a layer of granular material about 0.82 feet (0.25 meters) deep,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> and that the harpoons had not fired upon landing. After landing on the comet, <i>Philae</i> had been scheduled to commence its science mission, which included:\n</p>\n<ul><li>Characterisation of the nucleus</li>\n<li>Determination of the chemical compounds present, including amino acid <a href="/wiki/Enantiomer" title="Enantiomer">enantiomers</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meierhenrich2008book_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Meierhenrich2008book-90">[90]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Study of comet activities and developments over time</li></ul>\n<p>After bouncing, <i>Philae</i> settled in the shadow of a cliff,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Philaefound_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Philaefound-91">[91]</a></sup> canted at an angle of around 30 degrees. This made it unable to adequately collect solar power, and it lost contact with <i>Rosetta</i> when its batteries ran out after two days, well before much of the planned science objectives could be attempted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-skytel20141115_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-skytel20141115-15">[15]</a></sup> Contact was briefly and intermittently reestablished several months later at various times between 13 June and 9 July, before contact was lost once again. There was no communication afterwards,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> and the transmitter to communicate with <i>Philae</i> was switched off in July 2016 to reduce power consumption of the probe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> The precise location of the lander was discovered in September 2016 when <i>Rosetta</i> came closer to the comet and took high-resolution pictures of its surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Philaefound_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-Philaefound-91">[91]</a></sup> Knowing its exact location provides information needed to put Philae\'s two days of science into proper context.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Philaefound_91-2"><a href="#cite_note-Philaefound-91">[91]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_results">Notable results</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rosetta_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Notable results">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Comet_67P_on_31_January_2015_-_NAVCAM.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1797" data-file-width="1919" decoding="async" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Comet_67P_on_31_January_2015_-_NAVCAM.jpg/220px-Comet_67P_on_31_January_2015_-_NAVCAM.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Comet_67P_on_31_January_2015_-_NAVCAM.jpg/330px-Comet_67P_on_31_January_2015_-_NAVCAM.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Comet_67P_on_31_January_2015_-_NAVCAM.jpg/440px-Comet_67P_on_31_January_2015_-_NAVCAM.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Comet_67P_on_31_January_2015_-_NAVCAM.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The comet in January 2015 as seen by <i>Rosetta</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> NAVCAM</div></div></div>\n<p>Researchers expect the study of data gathered will continue for decades to come. One of the first discoveries was that the magnetic field of 67P oscillated at 40&#8211;50 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Millihertz" title="Millihertz">millihertz</a>. A German composer and sound designer created an artistic rendition from the measured data to make it audible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> Although it is a natural phenomenon, it has been described as a "song"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smithsonian20141112_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-smithsonian20141112-95">[95]</a></sup> and has been compared to <a href="/wiki/Continuum_(Ligeti)" title="Continuum (Ligeti)"><i>Continuum</i> for harpsichord</a> by <a href="/wiki/Gy%C3%B6rgy_Ligeti" title="Gy&#246;rgy Ligeti">Gy&#246;rgy Ligeti</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-classicfm20141114_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-classicfm20141114-96">[96]</a></sup> However, results from <i>Philae</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> landing show that the comet\'s nucleus has no magnetic field, and that the field originally detected by <i>Rosetta</i> is likely caused by the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esa20150414_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-esa20150414-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature20150414_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-nature20150414-98">[98]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Isotopic_signature" title="Isotopic signature">isotopic signature</a> of water vapour from comet 67P, as determined by the <i>Rosetta</i> spacecraft, is substantially different from that found on Earth. That is, the ratio of <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">deuterium</a> to <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> in the water from the comet was determined to be three times that found for terrestrial water. This makes it very unlikely that water found on Earth came from comets such as comet 67P, according to the scientists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20141210-DCA_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20141210-DCA-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20141210-KC_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20141210-KC-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC-20141211_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-20141211-101">[101]</a></sup> On 22 January 2015, NASA reported that, between June and August 2014, the rate at which water vapour was released by the comet increased up to tenfold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20150122_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20150122-102">[102]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 2 June 2015, NASA reported that the <a href="/wiki/Rosetta_(spacecraft)#Instruments" title="Rosetta (spacecraft)">Alice spectrograph</a> on <i>Rosetta</i> determined that <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a> within 1&#160;km (0.6&#160;mi) above the comet nucleus&#160;&#8212; produced from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photoionisation" title="Photoionisation">photoionisation</a> of water <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecules</a> by <a href="/wiki/Sunlight" title="Sunlight">solar radiation</a>, and not <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photons</a> from the Sun as thought earlier&#160;&#8212; are responsible for the degradation of water and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> molecules released from the comet nucleus into its <a href="/wiki/Coma_(cometary)" title="Coma (cometary)">coma</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20150602_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20150602-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AA-20150602_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-AA-20150602-104">[104]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="End_of_mission">End of mission</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rosetta_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: End of mission">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>As the orbit of comet 67P took it farther from the Sun, the amount of sunlight reaching <i>Rosetta</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> solar panels decreased. While it would have been possible to put <i>Rosetta</i> into a second hibernation phase during the comet\'s aphelion, there was no assurance that enough power would be available to run the spacecraft\'s heaters to keep it from freezing. To guarantee a maximum science return, mission managers made the decision to instead guide <i>Rosetta</i> down to the comet\'s surface and end the mission on impact, gathering photographs and instrument readings along the way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esa20160630_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-esa20160630-105">[105]</a></sup> On 23 June 2015, at the same time as a mission extension was confirmed, ESA announced that end of mission would occur at the end of September 2016 after two years of operations at the comet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esa20150623_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-esa20150623-106">[106]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="cquote pullquote" role="presentation" style="margin:auto; border-collapse: collapse; border: none; background-color: transparent; width: auto;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: top; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: \'Times New Roman\', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: left; padding: 10px 10px;">&#8220;\n</td>\n<td style="vertical-align: top; border: none; padding: 4px 10px;">All stations and the briefing room, we\'ve just had loss of signal at the expected time. This is another outstanding performance by flight dynamics. So we\'ll be listening for the signal from Rosetta for another 24 hours, but we don\'t expect any. This is the end of the Rosetta mission. Thank you, and goodbye.<br/>&#8212;Sylvain Lodiot, <i>Rosetta</i> Spacecraft Operations Manager, <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Operations_Centre" title="European Space Operations Centre">European Space Operations Centre</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sfnow20160930_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-sfnow20160930-107">[107]</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: bottom; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: \'Times New Roman\', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: right; padding: 10px 10px;">&#8221;\n</td></tr>\n</tbody></table>\n<p><i>Rosetta</i> began a 19&#160;km (12&#160;mi) descent with a 208-second thruster burn executed on 29 September 2016 at approximately 20:50&#160;<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UTC" title="UTC">UTC</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esa20160930_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-esa20160930-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eosjour20160930_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-eosjour20160930-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sfnow20160930_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-sfnow20160930-107">[107]</a></sup> Its trajectory targeted a site in the Ma\'at region near an area of dust- and gas-producing active pits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature20160930_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-nature20160930-110">[110]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Impact on the comet\'s surface occurred 14.5 hours after its descent manoeuvre; the final data packet from <i>Rosetta</i> was transmitted at 10:39:28.895&#160;UTC (<a href="/wiki/Spacecraft_Event_Time" title="Spacecraft Event Time">SCET</a>) by the OSIRIS instrument and was received at the <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Operations_Centre" title="European Space Operations Centre">European Space Operations Centre</a> in Darmstadt, Germany, at 11:19:36.541&#160;UTC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esa20160930_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-esa20160930-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eosjour20160930_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-eosjour20160930-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-twitter781816617097392129_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-twitter781816617097392129-111">[111]</a></sup> The spacecraft\'s estimated speed at the time of impact was 3.2&#160;km/h (2.0&#160;mph; 89&#160;cm/s),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-space20160930_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-space20160930-18">[18]</a></sup> and its touchdown location, named <i>Sais</i> by the operations team after the Rosetta Stone\'s original temple home, is believed to be only 40&#160;m (130&#160;ft) off-target.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature20160930_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-nature20160930-110">[110]</a></sup> The final complete image transmitted by the spacecraft of the comet was taken by its OSIRIS instrument at an altitude of 23.3&#8211;26.2&#160;m (76&#8211;86&#160;ft) about 10 seconds before impact, showing an area 0.96&#160;m (3.1&#160;ft) across.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature20160930_110-2"><a href="#cite_note-nature20160930-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esa20170928_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-esa20170928-112">[112]</a></sup> <i>Rosetta</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> computer included commands to send it into safe mode upon detecting that it had hit the comet\'s surface, turning off its radio transmitter and rendering it inert in accordance with <a href="/wiki/International_Telecommunication_Union" title="International Telecommunication Union">International Telecommunication Union</a> rules.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sfnow20160930_107-2"><a href="#cite_note-sfnow20160930-107">[107]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 28 September 2017, a previously unrecovered image taken by the spacecraft was reported. This image was recovered from three data packets discovered on a server after completion of the mission. While blurry due to data loss, it shows an area of the comet\'s surface approximately one square meter in size taken from an altitude of 17.9&#8211;21.0&#160;m (58.7&#8211;68.9&#160;ft), and represents <i>Rosetta</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> closest image of the surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esa20170928_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-esa20170928-112">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GZM-20170928_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-GZM-20170928-113">[113]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Instruments">Instruments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rosetta_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Instruments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For the instruments on <i>Philae</i>, see <a href="/wiki/Philae_(spacecraft)#Instruments" title="Philae (spacecraft)">Philae (spacecraft) &#167;&#160;Instruments</a>.</div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nucleus">Nucleus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rosetta_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Nucleus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The investigation of the nucleus was done by three <a href="/wiki/Optical_spectrometer" title="Optical spectrometer">optical spectrometers</a>, one <a href="/wiki/Microwave" title="Microwave">microwave</a> radio antenna and one <a href="/wiki/Radar" title="Radar">radar</a>:\n</p>\n<ul><li><b><a href="/wiki/Alice_(spacecraft_instrument)" title="Alice (spacecraft instrument)">Alice</a></b> (an ultraviolet imaging spectrograph). The <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> <a href="/wiki/Spectroscopy" title="Spectroscopy">spectrograph</a> searched for and quantified the <a href="/wiki/Noble_gas" title="Noble gas">noble gas</a> content in the comet nucleus, from which the temperature during the comet creation could be estimated. The detection was done by an array of <a href="/wiki/Potassium_bromide" title="Potassium bromide">potassium bromide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Caesium_iodide" title="Caesium iodide">caesium iodide</a> <a href="/wiki/Photocathode" title="Photocathode">photocathodes</a>. The 3.1&#160;kg (6.8&#160;lb) instrument used 2.9 watts, and an improved version is currently being used in the <a href="/wiki/New_Horizons" title="New Horizons">New Horizons</a> spacecraft. It operated in the extreme and far ultraviolet spectrum, from 700&#8211;2,050&#160;&#197; (70&#8211;205&#160;nm).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> ALICE was built and operated by the <a href="/wiki/Southwest_Research_Institute" title="Southwest Research Institute">Southwest Research Institute</a> for NASA\'s Jet Propulsion Laboratory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-swri20140610_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-swri20140610-116">[116]</a></sup></li>\n<li><b><a href="/wiki/Optical,_Spectroscopic,_and_Infrared_Remote_Imaging_System" title="Optical, Spectroscopic, and Infrared Remote Imaging System">OSIRIS</a></b> (Optical, Spectroscopic, and Infrared Remote Imaging System). The camera system had a <a href="/wiki/Telephoto_lens" title="Telephoto lens">narrow-angle</a> lens (700&#160;mm) and a <a href="/wiki/Wide-angle_lens" title="Wide-angle lens">wide-angle lens</a> (140&#160;mm), with a 2048&#215;2048 pixel <a href="/wiki/Charge-coupled_device" title="Charge-coupled device">CCD</a> chip. The instrument was constructed in Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup></li>\n<li><b>VIRTIS</b> (Visible and Infrared Thermal Imaging Spectrometer). The Visible and <a href="/wiki/Infrared_spectroscopy" title="Infrared spectroscopy">IR spectrometer</a> was able to make pictures of the nucleus in the IR and also search for IR spectra of molecules in the <a href="/wiki/Coma_(cometary)" title="Coma (cometary)">coma</a>. The detection was done by a mercury cadmium telluride array for IR and with a CCD chip for the <a href="/wiki/Visible_spectrum" title="Visible spectrum">visible wavelength</a> range. The instrument was produced in Italy, and improved versions were used for <a href="/wiki/Dawn_(spacecraft)" title="Dawn (spacecraft)">Dawn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Venus_Express" title="Venus Express">Venus Express</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup></li>\n<li><b>MIRO</b> (Microwave Instrument for the Rosetta Orbiter). The abundance and temperature of volatile substances like water, ammonia and carbon dioxide could be detected by MIRO via their <a href="/wiki/Microwave" title="Microwave">microwave</a> emissions. The 30&#160;cm (12&#160;in) radio antenna along with the rest of the 18.5&#160;kg (41&#160;lb) instrument was built by NASA\'s Jet Propulsion Laboratory with international contributions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mps.miro_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-mps.miro-119">[119]</a></sup></li>\n<li><b><a href="/wiki/CONSERT" title="CONSERT">CONSERT</a></b> (Comet Nucleus Sounding Experiment by Radiowave Transmission). The CONSERT experiment provided information about the deep interior of the comet using <a href="/wiki/Radar" title="Radar">radar</a>. The radar performed <a href="/wiki/Tomography" title="Tomography">tomography</a> of the nucleus by measuring electromagnetic wave propagation between the <i><a href="/wiki/Philae_(spacecraft)" title="Philae (spacecraft)">Philae</a></i> lander and the <i>Rosetta</i> orbiter through the comet nucleus. This allowed it to determine the comet\'s internal structure and deduce information on its composition. The electronics were developed by France and both antennas were constructed in Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup></li>\n<li><b>RSI</b> (Radio Science Investigation). RSI made use of the probe\'s communication system for physical investigation of the nucleus and the inner <a href="/wiki/Coma_(cometary)" title="Coma (cometary)">coma</a> of the comet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gas_and_particles">Gas and particles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rosetta_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Gas and particles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<ul><li><b>ROSINA</b> (Rosetta Orbiter Spectrometer for Ion and Neutral Analysis). The instrument consisted of a double-focus <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sector_instrument" title="Sector instrument">magnetic mass spectrometer</a> (DFMS) and a <a href="/wiki/Reflectron" title="Reflectron">reflectron</a> type <a href="/wiki/Time_of_flight" title="Time of flight">time of flight mass spectrometer</a> (RTOF). The DFMS had a high resolution (could resolve <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">N<sub>2</sub></a> from <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">CO</a>) for molecules up to 300 <a href="/wiki/Atomic_mass_unit" title="Atomic mass unit">amu</a>. The RTOF was highly sensitive for neutral molecules and for ions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> ROSINA was developed at the University of Bern in Switzerland.</li>\n<li><b><a href="/wiki/Micro-Imaging_Dust_Analysis_System" title="Micro-Imaging Dust Analysis System">MIDAS</a></b> (Micro-Imaging Dust Analysis System). The high-resolution <a href="/wiki/Atomic_force_microscopy" title="Atomic force microscopy">atomic force microscope</a> investigated several physical aspects of the dust particles which are deposited on a silicon plate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup></li>\n<li><b>COSIMA</b> (Cometary Secondary Ion Mass Analyser). COSIMA analysed the composition of dust particles by <a href="/wiki/Secondary_ion_mass_spectrometry" title="Secondary ion mass spectrometry">secondary ion mass spectrometry</a>, using <a href="/wiki/Indium" title="Indium">indium</a> ions. It could detect ions up to a mass of 6500&#160;amu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup></li>\n<li><b>GIADA</b> (Grain Impact Analyser and Dust Accumulator). GIADA analysed the dust environment of the <a href="/wiki/Coma_(cometary)" title="Coma (cometary)">comet coma</a> by measuring the optical cross section, momentum, speed and mass of each grain entering inside the instrument.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Solar_wind_interaction">Solar wind interaction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rosetta_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Solar wind interaction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<ul><li><b>RPC</b> (Rosetta Plasma Consortium).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Search_for_organic_compounds">Search for organic compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rosetta_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Search for organic compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Previous observations have shown that comets contain complex <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic compounds</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAQ_13-5"><a href="#cite_note-FAQ-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140221_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140221-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FromADistantComet_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-FromADistantComet-130">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tate_Jan2014_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tate_Jan2014-131">[131]</a></sup> These are the elements that make up <a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid" title="Nucleic acid">nucleic acids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a>, essential ingredients for life as we know it. Comets are thought to have delivered a vast quantity of water to Earth, and they may have also seeded Earth with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_molecule" title="Organic molecule">organic molecules</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kramer_UniToday_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kramer_UniToday-132">[132]</a></sup> <i>Rosetta</i> and <i>Philae</i> also searched for organic molecules, nucleic acids (the building blocks of <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> and <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a>) and amino acids (the building blocks of proteins) by sampling and analysing the comet\'s nucleus and coma cloud of gas and dust,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kramer_UniToday_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kramer_UniToday-132">[132]</a></sup> helping assess the contribution comets made to the <a href="/wiki/Abiogenesis" title="Abiogenesis">beginnings of life</a> on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAQ_13-6"><a href="#cite_note-FAQ-13">[13]</a></sup> Before succumbing to falling power levels, <i>Philae</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> COSAC instrument was able to detect organic molecules in the comet\'s atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-transient_contacts_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-transient_contacts-133">[133]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chirality_with_hands.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="519" data-file-width="765" decoding="async" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Chirality_with_hands.svg/220px-Chirality_with_hands.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Chirality_with_hands.svg/330px-Chirality_with_hands.svg.png 1.5x,
